الترويج لحل التوضيح والتلويح
رد الشارح: شاریخ نے دو وجہ سے رد کیا ہے (۱) مفہوم کلی شخصی کے ضمن میں پایا جاتا ہے مثال کے طور پر زید و عمرو بکر وغیرہ یہ اشخاص ہیں انسان کلی کے اور زید عمرو بکر میں انسان کا مفہوم کلی پایا جاتا ہے اور مانقل الینا یہ مفہوم کلی کی تعریف ہے اور ما کتب فیہ القرآن یہ مجموع شخصی ہے مجموع شخصی کے ضمن میں مفہوم کلی پایا گیا تو مفہوم کلی کا مجموع شخصی پر موقوف ہونا بعینہ مفہوم کلی پر موقوف ہونا ہے تو مفہوم کلی مفہوم کلی پر موقوف ہوا اور یہ دور ہے (۲) اگر تسلیم کرلیں کہ ان میں فرق ہے تو پھر ہم کہتے ہیں کہ یہ جو مصنف نے تعریف کی ہے ،انقل الینا کیسا تھ یہ مجموع شخصی کی ہے تو اب مجموعه شخصی کی معرفت مجموع مشخصی پر موقوف ہوگی کیونکہ مصحف کے معنی میں قرآن سے آپ خود مجموعه شخصی مراد لیتے ہیں تو یہ تو قف الشی علی نفسہ ہے جو دور ہے قَوْلُهُ ) بَلْ تَشْخِيصُهُ) أَى تَمْيِيرُهُ بِخَوَاصٌهِ فَإِنْ كَلِمَةَ أَي إِنَّمَا يُطْلَبُ بِهَا تميرُ الشَّيْء بِمَا يَخُصُّهُ شَخُصًا كَانَ أَوْ غَيْرَهُ .
قَوْلُهُ ) يُطْلَقُ عَلَى الْكَلَامِ الْأَزَلِيٌّ ( كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ عَلَيْهِ السَّلَامُ ( الْقُرْآنُ كَلَامُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى غَيْرٌ مَخْلُوقِ ) الْحَدِيث ، وَهُوَ صِفَةٌ قَدِيمَةٌ مُنَافِيَةٌ لِلسُّكُوتِ وَالْآفَةِ لَيْسَتْ مِنْ جِنسِ الْحُرُوفِ وَالْأَصْوَاتِ لَا تَخْتَلِفُ إِلَى الْأَمْرِ وَالنَّهْي وَالْإِخْبَارِ وَلَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِالْمَاضِي وَالْحَالِ وَالاسْتِقْبَالِ إِلَّا بِحَسَبِ التَّعَلَّقَاتِ وَالْإِضَافَاتِ كَالْعِلْمِ وَالْقُدْرَةِ وَسَائِرِ الصِّفَاتِ ، وَهَذَا الْكَلَامُ اللَّفْظِيُّ الْحَادِثِ الْمُؤَلَّفَ مِنْ الْأَصْوَاتِ وَالْحُرُوفِ الْقَائِمَةِ بِمَحَالْهَا يُسَمَّى كَلَامَ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى وَالْقُرْآنَ عَلَى مَعْنَى أَنَّهُ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ ذَلِكَ الْمَعْنَى الْقَدِيمِ إِلَّا أَنَّ الْأحْكامَ لَمَّا كَانَتْ فِي نَظَرِ الْأُصُولِيٌّ مَنُوطَةٌ بِالْكَلَامِ اللَّفْظِى دُونَ الْأَزَلِى جُعِلَ الْقُرْآنُ اسْمًا لَهُ وَاعْتُبِرَ فِي تَفْسِيرِهِ مَا يُمَيِّؤُهُ عَنْ الْمَعْنَى الْقَدِيمِ لَا يُقَالُ التَّمُسِيرُ يَحُصُلُ بِمُجَرَّدِ ذِكْرِ النَّقْلِ فَلَا حَاجَةَ إِلَى بَاقِي الْقُيُودِ ؛ لأنَّا نَقُولُ التَّعْرِيفُ ، وَإِنْ كَانَ لِلتَّمْيِيزِ لَا بُدَّ وَأَنْ يُسَاوِيَ الْمُعَرَّفَ فَذَكَرَ بَاقِيَ الْقُيُودِ لِتَحْصِيلِ الْمُسَاوَاةِ .
ترجمہ: یعنی ممتاز کرنا اس کو اس کے خواص کے ساتھ کیونکہ کلمہ اتنی طلب کیا جاتا ہے اس کے ذریعہ مٹی کی تمیز کو اس کے ساتھ جو خاص کر دے اس کو شخصی ہو یا غیر شخصی ہو۔
قوله يطلق على الكلام الازلی: اطلاق کیا جاتا ہے کلام ازلی پر جیسے آقاعلیہ السلام کے فرمان میں ہے کہ ، قرآن کلام اللہ ہے مخلوق نہیں ہے الحدیث اور وہ ایسی صفت ہے جو قدیم ہے منافی ہے سکوت اور آفت کے نہیں ہے حروف اور آوازوں کی جنس سے نہیں مختلف ہوتا امر و نہی اور اخبار کی طرف نہیں متعلق ہوتا ماضی حال و استقبال کے ساتھ مگر تعلقات اور اضافات کے اعتبار سے جیسا علم اور قدرت ہے اور یہ کلام لفظی جو حادث ہے مرکب ہے آوازوں سے اور ان حروف سے جو قائم ہیں اپنے محلوں کے ساتھ نام رکھا جاتا ہے ان کا کلام اللہ اور قرآن بناء کرتے ہوئے اس معنی پر کہ بیشک یہ عبارت ہے اس معنی قدیم سے مگر بیشک احکام جب تھے اصولی کی نظر میں متعلق کلام لفظی کے ساتھ نہ کہ کلام ازلی کے ساتھ تو بنایا قرآن کو نام اس ( لفظی) کا اور اعتبار کیا اس کی تفسیر میں اس چیز کا جو ممتاز کر دے اس کو معنی قدیم ہے۔ نہ اعتراض کیا جائے کہ تمیز تو حاصل ہو جاتی ہے محض نقل کے ذکر کرنے سے پس نہیں ہے کوئی ضرورت باقی قیود کی طرف کیونکہ ہم کہتے ہیں کہ تعریف اگر چہ تمیز کے لئے ہوتی ہے لیکن ضروری ہے کہ وہ مساوی ہو معرف کے پس باقی قیود کوذ کر کیا مساوات کو حاصل کرنے کے لئے۔
تشخیص کا معنی ومفہوم
تشریح : قولہ بل تشخیصہ : اس عبارت سے غرض شارع متن کی وضاحت ہے ماتن نے کہا تھا کہ یہ تعریف قرآن کی ہے اس کو ماعدا سے ممتاز کرنے کے لئے ہے ماہیت کی تعریف نہیں ہے کلام لفظی کی کلام ازلی سے تشخیص ہے شاریخ نے تشخیص کا معنی بیان کیا ہے کہ ایک شئی کی صفات ذکر کریں دوسری اشیاء سے ممتاز کرنے کے لئے اس کا نام تشخیص ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ کسی بئی کو ماعدا سے ممتاز کرنا ہو تو سوال اٹھی سے ہوتا ہے اور ائی سے سوال کا مقصد یہ ہوتا ہے کہ ایسی صفات ذکر کریں جو اس کو غیروں سے ممتاز کر دیں۔
قولہ مطلق: یہ عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے اس میں شاریخ نے تائید بیان کی ہے کہ قرآن کا اطلاق کلام از لی اور کلام نفسی دونوں پر ہوتا ہے حدیث ہے کہ قرآن اللہ کا کلام غیر مخلوق ہے معلوم ہوا کہ قرآن کا اطلاق جس طرح ہمارے کلام پر ہوتا ہے جو مخلوق ہے اسی طرح اللہ کے کلام پر بھی ہوتا ہے جو غیر مخلوق ہے۔
کلام از لی اور کلام لفظی میں فرق
وہی صفتہ قدیمہ : یہ عبارت کا دوسرا حصہ ہے اس میں شاریخ نے کلام از لی اور کلام لفظی کی تعریف بیان کر کے فرق بیان
The comment: The commentator refutes for two reasons: (1) The universal concept is found within the particular individuals, e.g., Zayd, Amr, Bakr are individuals of the universal 'human', and the universal concept of human is found in Zayd, Amr, Bakr. And 'what has been transmitted to us' is a definition of the universal concept, and 'what is written in the Quran' is a particular aggregate. Since the universal concept is found within the particular aggregate, the dependence of the universal concept on the particular aggregate is exactly dependence on the universal concept itself, resulting in the universal depending on itself, which is circular (dawr). (2) If we concede a difference between them, then we say: The definition given by the author, 'what has been transmitted to us', is of the particular aggregate. Thus, knowledge of the particular aggregate depends on the specified aggregate because you yourself take 'Quran' as the particular aggregate in the sense of the mushaf. This is the thing depending on itself, which is circular. His statement: 'Rather, its distinction (tashkhis),' i.e., distinguishing it by its specific properties, for the word 'ay' is used to seek distinguishing a thing by what is specific to it, whether it be a person or not. His statement: 'It is applied to the eternal speech,' as in his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated.' The hadith. And it is an eternal attribute, incompatible with silence and deficiency, not of the genus of letters and sounds, does not vary into command, prohibition, and information, and does not relate to past, present, or future except according to relations and attributions, like knowledge and power and the rest of the attributes. And this created verbal speech, composed of sounds and letters subsisting in their loci, is called the speech of Allah and the Quran in the sense that it is an expression of that eternal meaning, except that since rulings, in the view of the usuli, are attached to the verbal speech rather than the eternal, the Quran was made a name for it, and in its definition was considered what distinguishes it from the eternal meaning. One cannot object: Distinction is achieved merely by mentioning transmission, so there is no need for the remaining restrictions. Because we say: The definition, even if it is for distinction, must be coextensive with the defined; so the remaining restrictions were mentioned to achieve coextensiveness. Translation: i.e., distinguishing it by its specific properties, because the word 'ay' is sought by it for the distinction of a thing [؟] with what makes it specific, whether personal or non-personal. His statement: 'It is applied to the eternal speech': Application is made to the eternal speech, as in his (peace be upon him) statement: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated.' The hadith. And it is an eternal attribute, incompatible with silence and deficiency, not of the genus of letters and sounds, does not vary into command, prohibition, and information, and does not relate to past, present, or future except according to relations and attributions, like knowledge and power. And this created verbal speech, composed of sounds and the letters that subsist in their loci, is called the speech of Allah and the Quran, based on the meaning that it is an expression of that eternal meaning. But since rulings, in the view of the usuli, are attached to the verbal speech and not to the eternal speech, the Quran was made a name for the verbal speech, and in its interpretation was considered what distinguishes it from the eternal meaning. Do not object: Distinction is achieved merely by mentioning transmission, so there is no need for the remaining restrictions. Because we say: The definition, even if it is for distinction, must be coextensive with the defined; so the remaining restrictions were mentioned to achieve coextensiveness. The meaning and concept of tashkhis: Clarification: His statement 'Rather, its distinction': The purpose of this statement is the commentator's clarification of the text. The author had said that this definition of the Quran is for distinguishing it from other things, not a definition of its essence. It is a distinction of the verbal speech from the eternal speech. The commentator explained the meaning of tashkhis: to mention the attributes of a thing to distinguish it from other things; this is called tashkhis. The proof is that when one wants to distinguish a thing from other things, one uses the question 'ay' (which), and the purpose of the question 'ay' is to mention such attributes as will distinguish it from others. His statement 'Mutlaq': This is the first part of the statement, in which the commentator expresses support that the word 'Quran' applies to both the eternal speech and the mental speech (al-kalam al-nafsi). There is a hadith: 'The Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated.' It is understood that the application of 'Quran' is similar to its application to our speech, which is created, but also to Allah's speech, which is uncreated. The difference between eternal speech and verbal speech: The same eternal attribute: This is the second part of the statement, in which the commentator explains the definition of eternal speech and verbal speech and clarifies the difference.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is from the commentary on Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih, addressing the definition of the Quran. The commentator (sharih) begins by refuting two objections against the author's definition. The first objection concerns circularity: if the universal concept (like 'human') is found within particular individuals, then defining the Quran as 'what has been transmitted to us' (being a particular aggregate) would make the universal concept depend on itself since the universal is found in the particular. The second objection, even if one distinguishes between them, the author's definition makes knowledge of the particular aggregate depend on the specified aggregate (the mushaf), which is also circular. The commentator then explains the term 'tashkhis' (distinction) used by the author: it means to distinguish a thing by its specific properties, and the word 'ay' (which) is used to ask for such distinguishing features. Next, the commentator discusses the term 'Quran' as applied to eternal speech (kalam azali) and verbal speech (kalam lafzi). The eternal speech is an eternal attribute of Allah, not composed of letters or sounds, and not changing. The created verbal speech is called 'Quran' as an expression of that eternal meaning. Since Islamic legal rulings (ahkam) are attached to the verbal speech, the Quran is taken as a name for the verbal speech, and its definition must distinguish it from the eternal meaning. The commentator defends the necessity of additional restrictions beyond just 'transmitted' to achieve coextensiveness (musawaa) between the definition and the defined. The rest of the passage is an Urdu explanation reinforcing these points, including the concept of tashkhis as mentioning specific attributes for distinction, and confirming that 'Quran' is applied to both eternal and created speech, but for usul purposes, it denotes the verbal expression.

Key Terms
كلام أزلي (kalam azali): Eternal speech of Allah, an attribute that is pre-eternal, not composed of letters or sounds, and does not change.
كلام لفظي (kalam lafzi): The created verbal speech composed of sounds and letters that serves as an expression (ibara) of the eternal meaning.
عبارة (ibara): Expression; the verbal speech is an expression that points to the eternal meaning without being identical to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why does the definition of the Quran in usul al-fiqh focus on the verbal speech?
A) Because the verbal speech is uncreated
B) Because legal rulings are attached to the verbal speech
C) Because the eternal speech cannot be known
D) Because the majority of scholars agree

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the Quran is defined as the created verbal expression because it is the subject of legal rulings, while still affirming its connection to the eternal attribute.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Tashkhees and the Purpose of Defining the Quran
Explaining tashkhees and the dual purpose of definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the concept of tashkhees as it applies to the definition of the Quran and distinguish between kalam azali and kalam lafzi.

Core Idea
The definition of the Quran in usul al-fiqh is to diagnose verbal speech from eternal speech, not to capture its essence.

In usul al-fiqh, definitions often serve a diagnostic purpose rather than describing the inner nature of something. This lesson explores the commentator's clarification of the matn's definition of the Quran: it is a diagnosis (tashkhees) that distinguishes the verbal, created speech we recite from the eternal, uncreated attribute of Allah. Understanding this distinction is fundamental for properly deriving rulings from the Quranic text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the purpose of the definition
Recognize that the definition of the Quran given by the matn is not intended to capture the essence (mahiyyah) but to distinguish it from other things. The commentator explicitly says it is for tashkhees.
Tashkhees is a diagnostic definition.
2
Define tashkhees
Tashkhees means mentioning the attributes of a thing to distinguish it from others, similar to answering the question 'which?' with distinguishing features.
The linguistic proof uses the interrogative 'ayy' (which?).
3
Recognize the dual application of the term Quran
The word 'Quran' applies both to eternal speech (kalam azali) and to verbal speech (kalam lafzi). The hadith that the Quran is uncreated supports this dual usage.
This is crucial for usul: rulings are derived from the verbal speech.
4
Differentiate kalam azali and kalam lafzi
Eternal speech is free from letters, sounds, time, and linguistic categories; it is an uncreated attribute. Verbal speech is composed of letters and sounds, recited by creation, and created in time.
This distinction prevents theological confusion and clarifies the object of usul analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تشریح : قولہ بل تشخیصہ :۔ اس عبارت سے غرض شارع متن کی وضاحت ہے ماتن نے کہا تھا کہ یہ تعریف قرآن کی ہے اس کو ماعدا سے ممتاز کرنے کے لئے ہے ماہیت کی تعریف نہیں ہے کلام لفظی کی کلام ازلی سے تشخیص ہے شاریخ نے تشخیص کا معنی بیان کیا ہے کہ ایک شئی کی صفات ذکر کریں دوسری اشیاء سے ممتاز کرنے کے لئے اس کا نام تشخیص ہے دلیل یہ ہے کہ کسی بئی کو ماعدا سے ممتاز کرنا ہو تو سوال اٹھی سے ہوتا ہے اور ائی سے سوال کا مقصد یہ ہوتا ہے کہ ایسی صفات ذکر کریں جو اس کو غیروں سے ممتاز کر دیں۔ قولہ مطلق:۔ یہ عبارت کا پہلا حصہ ہے اس میں شاریخ نے تائید بیان کی ہے کہ قرآن کا اطلاق کلام از لی اور کلام نفسی دونوں پر ہوتا ہے حدیث ہے کہ قرآن اللہ کا کلام غیر مخلوق ہے معلوم ہوا کہ قرآن کا اطلاق جس طرح ہمارے کلام پر ہوتا ہے جو مخلوق ہے اسی طرح اللہ کے کلام پر بھی ہوتا ہے جو غیر مخلوق ہے۔ كلام از لي اور كلام لفظي ميں فرق وہی صفتہ قدیمہ :۔ یہ عبارت كا دوسرا حصہ ہے اس میں شاریخ نے کلام از لی اور کلام لفظی کی تعریف بیان کر کے فرق بیان كر ديا ہے كہ كلام از لي وہ ہے جو حروف سے آواز سے خاموش ہونے سے گونگا ہونے سے پاك ہو حروف سے پاك ہو كيونكہ غیر مخلوق ہے ماضی حال مستقبل سے خالي ہو كيونكہ اللہ کے لئے زمانہ نہیں ہے امر نہي و استفہام وغیرہ سے خالي ہو ہاں جب كلام كا تعلق مخلوق سے ہو تو حروف ، امر، نہی وغیرہ کے ساتھ تعبیر ہو اس کی کیفیت كيا ہے ؟ يہ اللہ جانتا ہے كلام لفظي وہ ہے جو مخلوق پڑھتی ہے امر نہی وغیرہ سے تعبیر ہو اور اصوات و حروف سے مركب ہے اور جس طرح ہم حادث ہيں يہ كلام بھی حادث ہے۔
Clarification: His statement 'but for diagnosis': The purpose of this phrase is to clarify the text of the matn. The author said that this definition of the Quran is to distinguish it from others; it is not a definition of essence but a diagnosis of verbal speech from eternal speech. The commentator explained the meaning of diagnosis: to mention the attributes of a thing to distinguish it from other things; this is called diagnosis. The proof is that when you want to distinguish something from others, the question comes with 'which?' and the purpose of asking 'which?' is to mention attributes that distinguish it from others. His statement 'absolute': This is the first part of the phrase; in it the commentator affirms that the word Quran applies both to eternal speech and to verbal speech. The hadith is that the Quran is the speech of Allah, uncreated. So it is known that the word Quran applies to our speech which is created and also to Allah's speech which is uncreated. Difference between eternal speech and verbal speech: The same ancient attribute: This is the second part of the phrase; in it the commentator defines eternal speech and verbal speech and explains the difference: Eternal speech is free from letters, sound, silence, muteness; free from letters because it is uncreated; free from past, present, future because Allah has no time; free from command, prohibition, question, etc. However, when speech relates to creation, it is expressed with letters, command, prohibition, etc. How that is? Allah knows. Verbal speech is what the creation recites, is expressed with command, prohibition, etc., is composed of sounds and letters, and just as we are created, this speech is also created.
Source pages p. 448

Commentary
This passage is an expansion from the commentator on the matn's phrase 'بل تشخیصہ' (but for diagnosis). The commentator explains that the definition of the Quran given in the matn is not a definition of the essence (mahiyyah) but a diagnosis (tashkhees) that distinguishes verbal speech (kalam lafzi) from eternal speech (kalam azali). Tashkhees is defined as mentioning the attributes of an object to distinguish it from others, as in the question 'ayy' (which?). The passage also clarifies that the word 'Quran' applies to both eternal speech (the uncreated attribute of Allah) and to verbal speech (the recited, created words). The commentator then distinguishes the two: eternal speech is free from letters, sounds, time, and linguistic categories like command and prohibition; it is an ancient attribute of Allah. Verbal speech is composed of letters and sounds, is recited by creation, and is created. This distinction is foundational for usul al-fiqh because the rulings (ahkam) are derived from the verbal speech (commands, prohibitions, etc.), not directly from the eternal speech.

Key Terms
التشخيص (al-tashkhees): Mentioning the attributes of a thing to distinguish it from others, as in the question 'ayy' (which?).
كلام ازلي (kalam azali): Eternal speech, an ancient attribute of Allah, free from letters, sounds, time, and linguistic categories.
كلام لفظي (kalam lafzi): Verbal speech composed of letters and sounds, recited by creation, and created in time.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the definition of the Quran given in the matn according to the commentator?
A) To define the essence (mahiyyah) of the Quran.
B) To distinguish verbal speech from eternal speech (tashkhees).
C) To prove the Quran is uncreated.
D) To list all attributes of the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The definition of the Quran in Hanafi usul al-fiqh is diagnostic (tashkhees), not essential, distinguishing verbal created speech from the eternal uncreated attribute of speech.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
10 min
Defining the Qur'an: Real vs. Nominal Definition in Hanafi Usul
Analysis of two definitions of Qur'an and preference for one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition (ḥadd ḥaqīqī) and a nominal definition (taʿrīf ismī/lafẓī) in the context of defining the Qur'an, and apply the Hanafi preference for the second probability to explain why the Qur'an cannot be defined by a comprehensive ḥadd.

Core Idea
The Qur'an is a unique individual (shakhṣī) that cannot be captured by a real definition; only a nominal description based on its specific composition is valid.

This passage from at-Tarwīḥ tackles a foundational issue in uṣūl al-fiqh: can the Qur'an be defined by a grammatical 'ḥadd'? The commentator explores two probabilities—defining it by the words uttered by Jibrīl or by its specific arrangement—and argues for the latter. As you will see, the preferred definition treats the Qur'an as a shakhṣī entity that can only be described nominally, a key distinction in Hanafi methodology that affects how we discuss the Qur'an's attributes and proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Probabilities
The passage presents two ways to define the Qur'an: (1) as the specific words uttered by Jibrīl (the first probability); (2) as the name of a specific arrangement of words, regardless of who recites it (the second probability). Determine each one and note their key features.
The first ties the Qur'an to a specific locus (Jibrīl's tongue); the second treats it as a universal concept.
2
Understand the Consequence of the First Probability
If the Qur'an is defined as Jibrīl's utterance, those words become accidents (aʿrāḍ) specific to that locus. This implies that our recitation would be only a likeness (mithl) of the Qur'an, not the Qur'an itself. Recognize why this is problematic for the belief in the Qur'an as the actual speech of Allah.
Accidents (aʿrāḍ) cannot exist in multiple loci without losing identity.
3
Follow the Commentator's Proof for Preferring the Second Probability
The commentator uses qiyās: just as a poem by Imru' al-Qays retains its identity as that poem regardless of who recites it, and just as sciences (e.g., logic) are defined by their specific rules not by the learners, so too the Qur'an is defined by its specific composition. Analyse how this analogy supports the second probability.
The poem example shows that the essence is the composition, not the reciter.
4
Grasp the Concept of Shakhṣī and its Implications for Definition
The passage explains that the Qur'an is a shakhṣī (individual) because it has unity in essence but multiplicity in locus (different reciters). A shakhṣī cannot have a real definition (ḥadd ḥaqīqī) because real definitions require a genus and differentia. Identify the two meanings of 'ḥadd' given: (1) impossibility of comprehensive knowledge except by recitation; (2) that it is shakhṣī in terminology.
Therefore, only nominal or verbal definitions (taʿrīf ismī/lafẓī) are possible, like 'the book between the two covers of the Mushaf'.
5
Evaluate the Critique of Ibn al-Ḥājib's Definition
Ibn al-Ḥājib defined the Qur'an as the composite individual (al-majmūʿ al-shaḥṣī). The commentator criticizes this as not being a real definition; it is merely nominal. Also, the potential circularity (defining Qur'an by surah) is rejected because knowledge of surah does not depend on knowledge of Qur'an but on revelation. Determine the Hanafi position: the only valid definitions are nominal, distinguishing the Qur'an from other books.
This usul dispute has no practical effect on the sanctity of the Qur'an but highlights the methodological rigor of Hanafi usul.

قَوْلُهُ ) فَإِنَّ الْأَعْرَاضَ تَنتَهِى ( أى تَبْلُعُ بِوَاسِطَةِ الْمُشَخْصَاتِ حَدًّا لَا يُمْكِنُ تَعَدُّدُهَا إِلَّا بِتَعَدُّدِ الْمَحَالَ كَقَوْلِ امْرِءِ الْقَيْسِ قِفَا نَبُكِ مِنْ ذِكْرَى حَبِيبٍ وَمَنْزِلٍ إلَى آخِرِ الْقَصِيدَةِ فَإِنَّهُ بِوَاسِطَةِ مُشَخْصَاتِهِ مِنَ التَّأْلِيفِ الْمَخْصُوصِ مِنَ الْحُرُوفِ وَالكَلِمَاتِ وَالأبْيَاتِ وَالْهَيْئَةِ الحَاصِلَةِ بالحَرَكَاتِ وَالسَّكْنَاتِ بَلَغَ حَدًا لَا يُمْكِنُ تعدده إلا بتعدد اللافِظِ حَتَّى إِذَا انْضَافَ إِلَيْهِ لِشَخْصِ اللَّافِطِ أَيْضًا يَصِيرُ شَخْصِيًّا حقيقيا لا يَتَعَدَدُ أَصْلًا فَالمُصَدِّفُ اصْطَلَحَ عَلَى تَسْمِيَةِ مِثْلٍ هَذَا الْمُؤَلْفِ شَخْصِيًّا قَبْلَ أَنْ يَنضَاف إلَيْهِ تَشَاصُ المَحَل وَيَصِيرُ شَخْصِيَّا حَقِيقِيًّا .
قَوْلُهُ ( وَقَدْ عَرَّفَ ابْنُ الْحَاجِبِ ( ظَاهِرُ تَعْرِيفِهِ لِلْمَجُمُوع الشَّعْمِي دُونَ المَفهومِ الكُلّى إِلَّا أَنْ يُقَالَ الْمُرَادُ بِسُورَةٍ مِنْ جِنْسِهِ فِي البَلاغَةِ وَالْقَصَاحَةِ وَعَلَى العُقَدِيرَينِ لُزُومُ الدَّوْرِ مَمْنُوعٌ ، لأنَّا لَا تُسَلَّمُ تَوَقَّفَ مَعْرِفَةِ مَنْهُومِ السُّورَةِ عَلَى مَعْرِفَةِ الْقُرْآنِ ، بَلْ هُوَ بَعْضٌ مُتَرْجَمَ أَوْلَهُ وَآخِرُهُ تَوْقِيفًا مِنْ كَلامٍ مُنَزِّلٍ قُرْآنًا كَانَ
The Commentary Resolving Al-Tawdih and Al-Talwih. The Qur'an does not accept a [grammatical] definition (hadd) because it is not possible to know its reality except by reciting it from beginning to end and by saying that it is words with that order. And of those two, the second interpretation is that (qā'im) becomes a term for naming such a composite that is not multiplied by the multiplication of loci, [being] personal (shakhṣī), and that a ruling be made that indeed it does not accept a definition because it is impossible to know its reality, except by pointing to it and by reading it from its beginning to its end, and it is not hidden that the discussion is about a real (ḥaqīqī) definition. Nevertheless, when discrimination is intended, it is possible in this manner: to say that the Qur'an is the collection that is transmitted between the two covers of the Mushaf alongside the revelation, just as it is said that al-Kashshāf is the book authored by 'Allāma Jār Allāh al-Zamakhsharī in the interpretation of the Qur'an, and [that] grammar (naḥw) is a science in which the states of words are discussed from the perspective of being i'rāb and binā'.

Reason for preferring the second probability: Tashrīḥ 'alā anna al-ḥaqq hādhā: This is the first part of the commentary's expression. The commentator's purpose is to explain the reason for preferring the second probability: There were two probabilities regarding the Qur'an—that the Qur'an is the name of the words that issued from the tongue of Jibrīl, or that it is the name of specific words [in] a specific order, whether the reciter be Zayd or 'Amr or anyone else. That is, the Qur'an is the name of a universal concept (mafhūm kullī). The author [al-Mui'n?] deemed the second probability preponderant. The commentator gave the proof for preference: If according to the first probability the Qur'an is the name of the words that came from the tongue of Jibrīl, then those words that came from Jibrīl's tongue are accidents (a'rāḍ), and accidents are specific to their locus (maḥall). So this Qur'an would be specific to Jibrīl's tongue. Then the Qur'an that the Prophet ﷺ taught and that we recite would not be the very Qur'an, but rather a likeness of the Qur'an. Therefore, the second probability should prevail: that the Qur'an is the name of a universal concept, and it is the name of a specific order, regardless of who recites it. He gave an example: just as poets recite poems (qaṣīda); whoever recites it, the poem is attributed to the poet. These poems are also the name of a specific order, whoever recites it. Similarly, the Qur'an is the name of specific words [in] a specific order, whoever recites it. Likewise, all sciences are names of specific rules; for example, the science of logic ('ilm al-ḥadd) is the name of specific rules; whether Zayd learns it or 'Amr sees it—that is, the multiplicity of learners does not cause multiplicity in those specific rules. Here, by relating to the impossible, multiplicity would occur in them, but unity would remain in essence.

So, based on this (fa-'alā hādha al-taqaddumī): This is the second part of the structure. The commentator of al-Tawḍīḥ states that since the Qur'an is the name of specific words [in] a specific order, whoever recites it, it is not haqīqī shakhṣī, because haqīqī shakhṣī is that which is one in terms of essence. Now, shakhṣī does not... [end of page 459]

[Page 460] What is meant by [the word] ḥadd? Two meanings have been stated: (1) The shakhṣī does not have a ḥadd, and it is known by indication; similarly, the Qur'an will not have a ḥadd, and it will be indicated. (2) Brother, a thing that has multiplicity in terms of locus but unity in all other respects—unity in terms of movements, resting states, and arrangement, only multiplicity in locus—when there is unity in all respects and multiplicity in only one respect, then in our terminology it is shakhṣī. Hence, the noble Qur'an is shakhṣī; it will not have a ḥadd. A shakhṣī matter cannot have a haqīqī definition, but it can have an ismī and lafẓī definition.

Look: This is the third part of the commentary's expression. The commentator's purpose is to explain a benefit: that all this detail is regarding the haqīqī definition—that a shakhṣī cannot have a haqīqī definition. Do not think that a lafẓī and ismī definition is also impossible; rather, a lafẓī and ismī definition can be made, as some people have defined [the Qur'an] as 'the Qur'an sent down to the Messenger...' etc. Similarly, 'transmitted between the two covers of the Mushaf alongside revelation,' just as al-Kashshāf is a commentary; it is said about it that it belongs to Jār Allāh al-Zamakhsharī, that Zamakhsharī wrote it. This is not a haqīqī definition, but rather to distinguish it from others.

His saying (qawluhu): 'Indeed, accidents (a'rāḍ) end (tantahtī)'—that is, they reach, by means of individuators (mushakhkhiṣāt), a limit that cannot be multiplied except by multiplying the loci, like the saying of Imru' al-Qays: 'Stop, let us weep at the remembrance of a beloved and a dwelling' to the end of the poem. For by means of its individuators—specific composition of letters, words, verses, and the form arising from movements and resting states—it reaches a limit that cannot be multiplied except by multiplying the speaker [i.e., the reciter]. So that, when the personality of the speaker is also added to it, it becomes a real shakhṣī (shakhṣī ḥaqīqī) that does not multiply at all. So the author of the matn [al-Mui'n?] coined the term to name such a composition shakhṣī before the individuation of the locus is added to it, making it real shakhṣī.

His saying (qawluhu): 'And Ibn al-Ḥājib defined—apparently his definition is for the composite individual (al-majmū' al-shaḥṣī), not the universal concept (al-mafhūm al-kullī), unless it is said that what is meant is a surah of its kind in eloquence and fluency. And on both estimates, the necessity of a vicious circle is denied, because we do not accept that the knowledge of the meaning of surah depends on the knowledge of the Qur'an; rather, it is a part whose beginning and end are determined by revelation from speech that is sent down as Qur'an.'
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage is a dispute over the correct definition of the Qur'an, specifically whether it is a haqīqī (real) definition or merely a lafẓī/ismī (verbal/nominal) one. The discussion arises from the claim that the Qur'an does not accept a grammatical 'hadd' (definition through genus and differentia) because it is a shakhṣī (individual/particular) entity. The commentator elaborates two probabilities: first, that the Qur'an is defined as the specific words uttered by Jibrīl (making it an accident tied to Jibrīl's locus); second, that it is defined as the specific arrangement of words regardless of who recites it. The second is preferred because if the Qur'an were tied to Jibrīl's utterance, our recitation would be merely a likeness, not the Qur'an itself. The example of a poem is given: a poem is defined by its specific composition, not by each recitation. Similarly, the Qur'an is a universal concept (mafhūm kullī) that retains its identity despite multiple reciters. The commentary then explains that a shakhṣī entity cannot have a real definition, but it can have a nominal definition (like 'the book between the covers of the Mushaf'). Finally, it critiques Ibn al-Ḥājib's definition for apparently defining the composite individual rather than the universal concept, and addresses the issue of circularity by arguing that knowledge of 'surah' does not depend on knowledge of 'Qur'an' but rather on revelation. Throughout, the author follows the Hanafi usul methodology, emphasizing the distinction between real and nominal definitions.

Key Terms
حد (ḥadd): A grammatical definition through genus and differentia that captures the essence of a thing; also used here more loosely as any comprehensive definition.
شخصی (shakhṣī): An individual entity that is unique and cannot be defined by a real definition; it is known by indication or nominal description.
تعریف حقیقی (taʿrīf ḥaqīqī): A real definition that reveals the essence of a thing through its genus and differentia.
تعریف اسمی / لفظی (taʿrīf ismī / lafẓī): A verbal or nominal definition that merely distinguishes the thing from others without revealing its essence.
اعراض (aʿrāḍ): Accidents (in philosophy); here, words uttered by Jibrīl are considered accidents that depend on a locus.
مفہوم کلی (mafhūm kullī): A universal concept that applies to multiple instances; the Qur'an as a concept is universal, not tied to a particular recitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator prefer the second probability (that the Qur'an is the name of specific words in a specific order) over the first (that it is the name of the words uttered by Jibrīl)?
A) Because the first probability would make the Qur'an an accident specific to Jibrīl's locus, so our recitation would not be the Qur'an itself.
B) Because the first probability is too narrow and does not include the entire Qur'an.
C) Because the second probability is easier to understand.
D) Because the second probability was approved by a majority of scholars.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an is a shakhṣī entity; it cannot be captured by a real definition, only by nominal descriptions like 'the specific composition of words transmitted between the covers of the Mushaf'.

Ibn al-Hajib's Definition of Quran: Collective Individual vs. Universal Concept
Critique of Ibn al-Hajib's definition and response to circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the collective individual (al-majmu' al-shakhsi) and universal concept (al-mafhum al-kulli) in defining the Quran, and explain why Ibn al-Hajib's definition avoids circularity according to the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Hajib's definition of Quran is not circular because 'surah' is known independently from other scriptures.

Ibn al-Hajib defined the Quran as 'revealed discourse for inimitability consisting of a surah from it'. This passage examines whether this definition refers to the Quran as a unique collective individual or as a universal concept. It also addresses the objection that defining Quran by surah leads to circularity, and shows that the objection fails because surah is a general term known from all revealed books.

Guided Path
1
Identify Ibn al-Hajib's definition
State Ibn al-Hajib's definition: 'al-kalam al-munazzal lil-i'jaz bi-suratin minhu' (revealed discourse for inimitability consisting of a surah from it).
This definition is presented and then analyzed.
2
Distinguish collective individual from universal concept
Understand that the word 'min' (from) is partitive, indicating that the definition applies to the whole Quran as a unique compound (majmu' shakhsi), not to the abstract concept (mafhum kulli). However, some interpret 'min' as 'from its genus', making it a definition for the universal concept.
This distinction affects whether the definition captures the uniqueness of the Quran.
3
Analyze the circularity objection
The objection: defining Quran by surah creates circularity because surah is part of Quran; one needs to know Quran to know surah. Recognize that this objection is based on assuming surah is specific to Quran.
The objection assumes mutual dependency.
4
Evaluate the response: surah is general
The response: 'surah' is a general term applicable to any revealed scripture (Torah, Psalms, Gospel). Its definition ('some revealed discourse from Allah with a beginning and an end') does not require prior knowledge of the Quran. Thus circularity is avoided.
This relies on the generality of 'surah' across all divine books.
5
Critique the alternative interpretation
The passage rejects an interpretation that would make the definition refer to the universal concept, arguing that it deviates from the well-known and apparent meaning ('some of the Quran') to an obscure one ('from its genus'). The Hanafi position upholds the definition for the collective individual.
This shows the importance of sticking to the apparent meaning (zahir) in definitions.

Or other [scriptures] by the evidence of the surahs of the Gospel and the Psalms. For this reason he needed his statement 'by a surah from it' i.e., from that revealed discourse, so understand. Translation: His statement 'fa-inna al-a'rad' meaning they reach through their specifics to such an extent that their multiplicity is not possible except by multiplicity of loci, like the statement of Imru' al-Qais 'qada sabaka min dhikra habib wa manzil' to the end of the poem because it has reached through its specific characteristics (the specific composition of letters, words, verses, and the condition of motions and pauses) to the extent that its multiplicity is not possible except by multiplicity of speakers until when the speaker's identity is added to it, it becomes true individual, not multiple. So the author established a term for this compound as 'individual' before the locus's identity is added and it becomes truly individual. His statement 'wa qad 'arrafa Ibn al-Hajib' means that his definition apparently refers to the collective individual, not the universal concept. But it could be said that what is meant is a surah from the genus of that discourse in eloquence and fluency. On both interpretations, the circularity is prevented because we do not accept that understanding the concept of surah depends on understanding the Quran. Rather, the surah is a part that is marked by its beginning and end, determined from revealed discourse whether Quran or other, by the evidence of the surahs of the Gospel and the Psalms. For this reason, the author needed his statement 'by a surah from it' i.e., from that revealed discourse, so understand. Commentary: 'fa-inna al-a'rad': The text is explained with an example: the author said that a'rad reach through specifics to such an extent that specificity is achieved from all aspects, and possibility of multiplicity remains only in the locus. For example, the poem of Imru' al-Qais 'qada'ika' addressing himself and his mount when he passed by the beloved's abodes. This poem, by specific characteristics (specific composition of specific letters, words, verses, and the condition of motions and pauses), has reached such an extent that there is unity in all aspects; no multiplicity remains in its essence except by the speaker and the locus. Then the author says: if there is unity in all other aspects and multiplicity only in the locus, then in our terminology it is 'pre-individual'. besturdubooks.wordpress.com Al-Tarwīḥ li-Ḥall al-Tawḍīḥ wa al-Talwīḥ Is the definition of Ibn al-Hajib free from objection? 461 Commentary: 'qawluhu wa qad 'arrafa Ibn al-Hajib': Ibn al-Hajib defined the Quran as 'the revealed discourse for inimitability consisting of a surah from it'. The commentator (Taftazani?) said this definition is for the collective individual of the Quran, not the universal concept, because the word 'min' in the definition is partitive, and partitive applies to the whole, not the universal concept. That is, the Quran is a discourse that is revealed and consists of a part of it. But by interpretation, this definition can be made for the universal concept. If we make it for the universal concept, the phrase would be 'by a surah from its genus' i.e., 'min' must be followed by the word 'jins'. Then the commentator said that the circularity objection does not necessarily apply whether it is a definition of the individual or the universal concept because the word 'surah' is general; its definition is 'some revealed discourse from Allah with a beginning and an end and marked with a title'. The word 'revealed' applies to Torah, Psalms, Gospel, and Quran, so surah is a part of the revealed discourse, whether Torah, Gospel, etc. So surah is general. Therefore, understanding the Quran depends on surah, but understanding surah does not depend on the Quran; it is not a vicious circle. Hence circularity does not occur. Response: The commentator's interpretation is incorrect because it deviates from the well-known to the unknown, from the apparent to the hidden. The well-known and apparent definition was 'some of the Quran', but the commentator's definition is unknown and hidden, so it is not correct.
Source pages p. 461

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the definition of the Quran according to Ibn al-Hajib and the response to an objection of circularity. The author begins by explaining the concept of 'a'rad' (accidents) with an example from Imru' al-Qais's poem to illustrate how a particular composition becomes unique and non-multiple except in terms of the speaker. Then, he presents Ibn al-Hajib's definition of the Quran as 'revealed discourse for inimitability consisting of a surah from it'. The commentator (Taftazani) critiques this definition, pointing out that the word 'min' (from) indicates partitivity, so the definition applies to the collective individual (al-majmu' al-shakhsi) not the universal concept (al-mafhum al-kulli). However, he allows that by interpreting 'min' as 'from the genus', the definition could be for the universal concept. The main objection is circularity: defining Quran by surah, and surah requires knowing Quran. The commentator rejects this because surah is a broader term applicable to any revealed scripture (Torah, Psalms, etc.), so its definition does not depend on the Quran. The passage concludes with a response that the commentator's interpretation is not correct because it deviates from the well-known definition (ba'd min al-Quran) to an obscure one. The Hanafi position here is to uphold the definition as referring to the collective individual, which avoids the circularity issue.

Key Terms
المجموع الشخصي (al-majmu' al-shakhsi): The collective individual: the specific instance of a compound that is unique and not repeatable across different contexts
المفهوم الكلي (al-mafhum al-kulli): The universal concept: the abstract idea of Quran that applies to any instance
دور (dawr): Circularity: a logical fallacy in definition where each term depends on the other

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does Ibn al-Hajib's definition of the Quran not fall into circularity?
A) Because the definition is only for the collective individual, not the universal concept.
B) Because the word 'surah' is general and can be known from other revealed scriptures, not requiring prior knowledge of the Quran.
C) Because the definition uses 'min' in a partitive sense, making it clear.
D) Because the commentator's interpretation is accepted by all.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Hajib's definition of the Quran, understood as referring to the collective individual, avoids circularity because the term 'surah' is independently known from other revealed scriptures.

Nazm and the Necessity of Arabic Recitation
Defining nazm and addressing an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether nazm (Arabic arrangement) is required for valid recitation in prayer and identify the mufta bihi after Abu Hanifa's retraction.

Core Idea
The author uses 'nazm' for respect; Abu Hanifa's initial view that nazm is not essential in prayer was retracted.

This lesson begins with the author's careful choice of terminology. Since the Quran is a sacred text, the author avoids the mundane word 'lafz' and uses 'nazm' (ordered speech). He then addresses a reported view of Imam Abu Hanifa that nazm is not required for the validity of prayer, but clarifies that this view was retracted. Understanding this dispute is crucial because it affects the ruling on reciting the Quran in translation during prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term 'nazm' and its rationale
Locate where the author explains why he uses 'nazm' instead of 'lafz'. What is the reason given?
The author says 'lafz' implies spitting, so it's disrespectful for the Quran.
2
Extract Abu Hanifa's initial position
Find the phrase that describes Abu Hanifa's view on nazm in prayer. What did he consider sufficient?
He considered only meaning, so Persian recitation was valid.
3
Note the retraction (ruju')
The author states that the more correct view (al-sah) is that Abu Hanifa retracted. What is the implication?
Now nazm is required for valid prayer.
4
Determine the practical consequence
What is the thamarah al-khilaf? In practice, if someone prays in Persian without excuse, what is the ruling?
Prayer is invalid according to the later position.

آيَةً مِنَ الْقُرْآنِ بِالْفَارِسِيَّةِ يَجُوزُ ؛ لأنَّهُ لَيْسَ بِقُرآنِ لِعَدَمِ النَّظْمِ لَكِنَّ الْأَصَحَ أَنَّهُ رَجَعَ عَنْ هَذَا الْقَوْلِ أَى :عَنْ عَدَمِ لُزُومِ النَّظْمِ فِي حَقِّ جَوَازِ الصَّلَاةِ فَلِهَذَا لَمْ أُورِدُ هَذَا الْقَوْلَ فِي الْمَتْنِ ، بَلْ قُلْت إِنَّ الْقُرْآنَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ النَّظْمِ الدَّالِّ عَلَى الْمَعْنَى وَمَشَايِخُنَا قَالُوا : إنَّ القُرْآنَ هُوَ النَّظْمُ وَالْمَعْنَى ، وَالظَّاهِرُ أَنَّ مُرَادَهُمُ النَّظْمُ الدَّالُ عَلَى الْمَعْنَى فَاخْتَرْت هَذِهِ الْعِبَارَةَ . ) بِاعْتِبَارِ وَضْعِهِ لَهُ هَذَا هُوَ التَّقْسِيمُ الْأَوَّلُ مِنَ التَّقَاسِيمِ الأرْبَعَةِ فَيَنْقَسِمُ الْكَلَامُ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْوَضْعِ إِلَى الْخَاصٌ وَالْعَامِّ ، وَالْمُشْتَرَكِ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي ، وَهَذَا مَا قَالَ فَخُرُ الْإِسْلَامِ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ الأَوَّلُ فِي وُجُوهِ النَّظْمِ صِيغَةٌ وَلْغَةٌ ) ثُمَّ بِاعْتِبَارِ اسْتِعْمَالِهِ فِيهِ ) هَذَا هُوَ التَّقْسِيمُ الثَّانِي فَيَنْقَسِمُ اللّفظ باعتبار الاستعمال أَنَّهُ مُسْتَعْمَلْ فِي الْمَوْضُوعِ لَهُ أَوْ فِي غَيْرِهِ كَمَا يَجِيءُ . ) ثُمَّ بِاعْتِبَارِ ظُهُورِ الْمَعْنَى عَنْهُ وَخَفَائِهِ وَمَرَاتِبِهِمَا ) ، وَهَذَا مَا قَالَ فَخُرُ الْإِسْلَامِ وَالثَّانِي فِي وُجُوهِ الْبَيَانِ بِذَلِكَ النَّظْمِ ، وَإِنَّمَا جَعَلْت هَذَا التَّقْسِيمَ ثَالِثًا وَاعْتِبَارُ الاسْتِعْمَالِ ثَانِيَا عَلَى عَكْسِ، مَا أَوْرَدَهُ فَخُرُ الْإِسْلَامِ ؛ لِأَنَّ الْاسْتِعْمَالَ مُقَدَّمٌ عَلَى ظُهُورِ الْمَعْنَى وَخَفَائِهِ ) ثُمَّ فِى كَيْفِيَّةِ دَلَالَتِهِ عَلَيْهِ ) ، وَهَذَا مَا قَالَ فَخُرُ الْإِسْلَامِ وَالرَّابِعُ فِي وُجُوهِ الْوُقُوفِ عَلَى أَحْكَامِ النَّظْمِ . ترجمہ:۔ (پہلا باب جب ہے قرآن نظم دال علی معنی تو تقسیم کیا گیالفظ کو نسبت کرتے ہوئے معنی کی طرف چار تقسیموں میں ) مراد نظم سے یہاں لفظ ہے مگر بیشک لفظ کا اطلاق کرنے میں قرآن پر ایک قسم کی سوء ادبی ہے کیونکہ لفظ اصل میں منہ سے کسی شئی کو گرانا ہے پس اسی وجہ سے اختیار کیا نظم کو لفظ کی جگہ اور تحقیق روایت کیا گیا ہے امام ابو حنیفہ سے کہ بیشک انہوں نے نہیں بنایا نظم کو رکن لازم نماز کے جائز ہونے کے حق میں خاص طور پر بلکہ اعتبار کیا ہے معنی کا فقط حتی کہ اگر قراءت کی غیر عربی کی نماز کے اندر بغیر عذر کے تو جائز ہے نماز امام صاحب کے ہاں اور جز این نیست کہ کہا ہے خاصہ کیونکہ انہوں نے بنایا اس کو رکن
The first chapter: Since the Quran is nazm (ordered speech) indicating meaning, the word (lafz) is divided in relation to meaning into four divisions. (What is meant by nazm here is lafz, except that applying the word lafz to the Quran involves a kind of disrespect, because lafz originally means 'to drop something from the mouth'; therefore he chose nazm in place of lafz. And it has been narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that he did not consider nazm an essential pillar specifically for the validity of prayer, but rather considered only the meaning, so much so that if one recited in other than Arabic in prayer without excuse, the prayer is valid according to him. He said 'specifically' because he considered it necessary in other than the validity of prayer, like the recitation of a junub or menstruating woman; even if one recites a verse of the Quran in Persian, it is permitted, because it is not Quran due to the lack of nazm. But the more correct view is that he retracted this opinion, i.e., regarding the non-necessity of nazm for the validity of prayer. Therefore I did not mention this opinion in the text, but rather I said that the Quran is an expression of nazm that indicates meaning. Our mashayikh said: The Quran is nazm and meaning. The apparent meaning is that they intend nazm that indicates meaning, so I chose this wording. Regarding its wad' (coinage) for its meaning: this is the first of the four divisions. Speech is divided according to wad' into khas (specific), 'amm (general), and mushtarak (homonym), as will come. This is what Fakhr al-Islam, may Allah have mercy on him, said: the first in aspects of nazm is form and language. Then regarding its isti'mal (usage) in its meaning: this is the second division. The word is divided according to usage into being used in its original meaning or in other than it, as will come. Then regarding the clarity of the meaning from it and its concealment and their levels: this is what Fakhr al-Islam said: the second in aspects of bayan (elucidation) by that nazm. However I made this division third and the consideration of usage second, contrary to what Fakhr al-Islam presented, because usage precedes clarity and concealment of meaning. Then regarding the kayfiyya (manner) of its indication upon it: this is what Fakhr al-Islam said: the fourth in aspects of stopping upon the rulings of nazm. Translation: (The first chapter: When the Quran is nazm indicating meaning, the word is divided in relation to meaning into four divisions. What is meant by nazm here is lafz, but applying lafz to the Quran is a kind of disrespect because lafz originally means to drop something from the mouth, so nazm was chosen in place of lafz. And indeed it is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that he did not make nazm a necessary pillar specifically for the validity of prayer, but rather considered only the meaning, so that if one recited in non-Arabic in prayer without excuse, the prayer is valid according to him. And he said 'specifically' because he made it necessary in other than the validity of prayer, such as the recitation of a junub and menstruating woman. He made it a pillar...)
Source pages p. 466

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the first chapter of the book, which explains the fourfold division of lafz (word) in relation to meaning. The author first clarifies why he uses the term 'nazm' (ordered speech) instead of 'lafz' for the Quran: to avoid disrespect, since 'lafz' literally means 'to spit out'. He then mentions a reported view of Imam Abu Hanifa that nazm is not essential for the validity of prayer, but notes that the more correct view is that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted this opinion, so the author did not include it in the matn. Instead, the author defines the Quran as 'nazm that indicates meaning'. The passage then lists the four divisions: (1) by wad' (coinage) into khas (specific), 'amm (general), and mushtarak (homonym); (2) by isti'mal (usage) into haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative); (3) by clarity and concealment of meaning (e.g., zahir, nass); (4) by manner of indication (dalalah). The author explains why he reordered Fakhr al-Islam's sequence: usage precedes clarity. This classification is foundational in Hanafi usul for analyzing Quranic and other texts.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): Ordered speech; synonym for lafz but preferred for Quran to show respect.
وضع (wad'): Original coinage of a word for a meaning.
استعمال (isti'mal): Usage of a word in its original or transferred sense.
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction of a former legal opinion by a mujtahid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author of this passage, what is the operative (mufta bihi) position regarding recitation in a language other than Arabic in prayer without excuse?
A) The prayer is valid because meaning is what matters
B) The prayer is invalid because nazm is required
C) It is permissible but makruh
D) It depends on the intention of the reciter

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that Arabic recitation (nazm) is required for valid prayer, based on the retraction of Abu Hanifa's earlier view.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
beginner
10 min
The Fourfold Division of Speech in Hanafi Usul
Introducing the fourfold taxonomy of lafz

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four divisions of lafz in relation to meaning and explain the author's reordering of the second and third divisions compared to Fakhr al-Islam.

Core Idea
Speech is analyzed through four lenses: coinage, usage, clarity, and manner of indication; usage must be assessed before clarity.

After clarifying the term nazm, the author presents the fourfold classification of lafz (speech) in relation to meaning. These divisions are foundational in Hanafi usul for analyzing any legal text. The author also explains why he reorders the sequence presented by Fakhr al-Islam: since understanding a word's usage is a prerequisite for evaluating its clarity, the division based on usage comes before the division based on clarity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first division (by wad')
Find the first division mentioned. What are the categories? The text says speech is divided by wad' into khas, 'amm, and mushtarak.
This will be detailed in later chapters.
2
Identify the second division (by isti'mal)
What is the basis of the second division? It concerns whether a word is used in its original meaning (haqiqah) or a transferred meaning (majaz).
Usage must be determined before assessing clarity.
3
Identify the third division (by clarity and concealment)
What does the third division categorize? It concerns the clarity of the meaning (zahir, nass, etc.) and levels of concealment (khafi, mushkil, etc.).
This is discussed under 'wujuh al-bayan'.
4
Identify the fourth division (by manner of indication)
What is the fourth division? It concerns how the word indicates its meaning (dalalah) — e.g., explicitly or implicitly.
Covered under 'wujuh al-wuquf 'ala ahkam al-nazm'.
5
Explain the reordering
Why does the author place the division by usage second and the division by clarity third, contrary to Fakhr al-Islam? The author argues that usage precedes clarity because you must first know in what sense the word is used before evaluating how clear that meaning is.
This reflects a methodological priority in usul analysis.

The first chapter: Since the Quran is nazm (ordered speech) indicating meaning, the word (lafz) is divided in relation to meaning into four divisions. (What is meant by nazm here is lafz, except that applying the word lafz to the Quran involves a kind of disrespect, because lafz originally means 'to drop something from the mouth'; therefore he chose nazm in place of lafz. And it has been narrated from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, that he did not consider nazm an essential pillar specifically for the validity of prayer, but rather considered only the meaning, so much so that if one recited in other than Arabic in prayer without excuse, the prayer is valid according to him. He said 'specifically' because he considered it necessary in other than the validity of prayer, like the recitation of a junub or menstruating woman; even if one recites a verse of the Quran in Persian, it is permitted, because it is not Quran due to the lack of nazm. But the more correct view is that he retracted this opinion, i.e., regarding the non-necessity of nazm for the validity of prayer. Therefore I did not mention this opinion in the text, but rather I said that the Quran is an expression of nazm that indicates meaning. Our mashayikh said: The Quran is nazm and meaning. The apparent meaning is that they intend nazm that indicates meaning, so I chose this wording. Regarding its wad' (coinage) for its meaning: this is the first of the four divisions. Speech is divided according to wad' into khas (specific), 'amm (general), and mushtarak (homonym), as will come. This is what Fakhr al-Islam, may Allah have mercy on him, said: the first in aspects of nazm is form and language. Then regarding its isti'mal (usage) in its meaning: this is the second division. The word is divided according to usage into being used in its original meaning or in other than it, as will come. Then regarding the clarity of the meaning from it and its concealment and their levels: this is what Fakhr al-Islam said: the second in aspects of bayan (elucidation) by that nazm. However I made this division third and the consideration of usage second, contrary to what Fakhr al-Islam presented, because usage precedes clarity and concealment of meaning. Then regarding the kayfiyya (manner) of its indication upon it: this is what Fakhr al-Islam said: the fourth in aspects of stopping upon the rulings of nazm. Translation: (The first chapter: When the Quran is nazm indicating meaning, the word is divided in relation to meaning into four divisions. What is meant by nazm here is lafz, but applying lafz to the Quran is a kind of disrespect because lafz originally means to drop something from the mouth, so nazm was chosen in place of lafz. And indeed it is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that he did not make nazm a necessary pillar specifically for the validity of prayer, but rather considered only the meaning, so that if one recited in non-Arabic in prayer without excuse, the prayer is valid according to him. And he said 'specifically' because he made it necessary in other than the validity of prayer, such as the recitation of a junub and menstruating woman. He made it a pillar...)

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the first chapter of the book, which explains the fourfold division of lafz (word) in relation to meaning. The author first clarifies why he uses the term 'nazm' (ordered speech) instead of 'lafz' for the Quran: to avoid disrespect, since 'lafz' literally means 'to spit out'. He then mentions a reported view of Imam Abu Hanifa that nazm is not essential for the validity of prayer, but notes that the more correct view is that Imam Abu Hanifa retracted this opinion, so the author did not include it in the matn. Instead, the author defines the Quran as 'nazm that indicates meaning'. The passage then lists the four divisions: (1) by wad' (coinage) into khas (specific), 'amm (general), and mushtarak (homonym); (2) by isti'mal (usage) into haqiqah (literal) and majaz (figurative); (3) by clarity and concealment of meaning (e.g., zahir, nass); (4) by manner of indication (dalalah). The author explains why he reordered Fakhr al-Islam's sequence: usage precedes clarity. This classification is foundational in Hanafi usul for analyzing Quranic and other texts.

Key Terms
خاص (khas): A word that indicates a single specific meaning.
عام ('amm): A word that indicates multiple instances without restriction.
مشترك (mushtarak): A homonym: one word with two or more unrelated meanings.
حقيقة (haqiqah): A word used in its originally coined meaning.
مجاز (majaz): A word used in a transferred sense due to a connection.
ظاهر (zahir): A word whose meaning is clear but open to ta'wil (interpretation).
نص (nass): A word whose meaning is clear and not open to ta'wil.
دلالة (dalalah): The manner in which a word indicates its meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author place the division by usage (isti'mal) before the division by clarity (zuhur), contrary to Fakhr al-Islam's sequence?
A) Because usage is more important than clarity
B) Because one cannot assess clarity without first knowing how the word is used
C) Because clarity is a subset of usage
D) Because the author prefers to follow a different scholar

Key Takeaway
The fourfold division provides a comprehensive analytical framework for Islamic legal texts, with usage logically preceding clarity in analysis.

The Four Divisions of Speech in Hanafi Usul
Defining the fourfold division of kalam by wad', isti'mal, zuhur, and dalala

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four considerations of kalam (wad', isti'mal, zuhur, kayfiyya dalala) and classify a given word according to these divisions.

Core Idea
Speech is analyzed from four angles: placement, usage, clarity, and indication — each leading to different legal categories.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding how words relate to meanings is foundational for extracting rulings from the divine sources. This passage presents the fourfold framework established by Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi, showing that a single word can be studied from different perspectives: its original assignment (wad'), its actual use (isti'mal), its clarity or ambiguity (zuhur/khafa), and the manner it points to its meaning (kayfiyya dalala). Mastering this classification will help you navigate later discussions on 'amm, khass, mushtarak, zahir, nass, and other key concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four dimensions
Read the passage and list the four 'considerations' (i'tibar) the author uses to divide speech: by wad' (placement), by isti'mal (usage), by zuhur/khafa (clarity/obscurity), and by kayfiyya dalala (manner of indication). Notice that the author reorders the second and third compared to Fakhr al-Islam.
The reordering is because usage logically precedes clarity.
2
Focus on the first division: wad'
Under wad', the author mentions three types: khass (specific), 'amm (general), and mushtarak (shared). Understand the basic definition of each in your own words.
These will be expanded in later lessons.
3
Focus on the second division: isti'mal
This division concerns whether a word is used in its original meaning or in some other meaning (figurative). Recognize that usage can be haqiqi (literal) or majazi (figurative).
The passage says 'as will come', so details are deferred.
4
Focus on the third division: zuhur/khafa
This considers degrees of clarity and obscurity in the meaning conveyed by the nazm. This leads to categories like zahir, nass, mujmal, etc.
The author places this third, after usage.
5
Focus on the fourth division: kayfiyya dalala
This examines the manner in which the speech indicates its meaning, ultimately leading to knowing the ahkam (rulings) derived from that nazm.
This is the most practical division, linking directly to istinbat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بِاعْتِبَارِ وَضْعِهِ لَهُ هَذَا هُوَ التَّقْسِيمُ الْأَوَّلُ مِنَ التَّقَاسِيمِ الأرْبَعَةِ فَيَنْقَسِمُ الْكَلَامُ بِاعْتِبَارِ الْوَضْعِ إِلَى الْخَاصٌ وَالْعَامِّ ، وَالْمُشْتَرَكِ كَمَا سَيَأْتِي ، وَهَذَا مَا قَالَ فَخْرُ الْإِسْلَامِ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ الأَوَّلُ فِي وُجُوهِ النَّظْمِ صِيغَةٌ وَلْغَةٌ ) ثُمَّ بِاعْتِبَارِ اسْتِعْمَالِهِ فِيهِ ) هَذَا هُوَ التَّقْسِيمُ الثَّانِي فَيَنْقَسِمُ اللّفظ باعتبار الاستعمال أَنَّهُ مُسْتَعْمَلْ فِي الْمَوْضُوعِ لَهُ أَوْ فِي غَيْرِهِ كَمَا يَجِيءُ . ) ثُمَّ بِاعْتِبَارِ ظُهُورِ الْمَعْنَى عَنْهُ وَخَفَائِهِ وَمَرَاتِبِهِمَا ) ، وَهَذَا مَا قَالَ فَخْرُ الْإِسْلَامِ وَالثَّانِي فِي وُجُوهِ الْبَيَانِ بِذَلِكَ النَّظْمِ ، وَإِنَّمَا جَعَلْت هَذَا التَّقْسِيمَ ثَالِثًا وَاعْتِبَارُ الاسْتِعْمَالِ ثَانِيَا عَلَى عَكْسِ، مَا أَوْرَدَهُ فَخْرُ الْإِسْلَامِ ؛ لِأَنَّ الْاسْتِعْمَالَ مُقَدَّمٌ عَلَى ظُهُورِ الْمَعْنَى وَخَفَائِهِ ) ثُمَّ فِى كَيْفِيَّةِ دَلَالَتِهِ عَلَيْهِ ) ، وَهَذَا مَا قَالَ فَخْرُ الْإِسْلَامِ وَالرَّابِعُ فِي وُجُوهِ الْوُقُوفِ عَلَى أَحْكَامِ النَّظْمِ .
By consideration of its placement for it, this is the first of the four divisions. So speech divides by consideration of placement into the specific, the general, and the shared, as will come. And this is what Fakhr al-Islam said: the first concerning the aspects of nazm, form and language. Then by consideration of its usage in it: this is the second division. So the word divides by consideration of usage into that it is used in what it was placed for or in other than it, as will come. Then by consideration of the manifestation of meaning from it and its concealment and their ranks: and this is what Fakhr al-Islam said: the second concerning the aspects of clarification by that nazm. And I made this division third and consideration of usage second contrary to what Fakhr al-Islam presented, because usage precedes manifestation and concealment of meaning. Then regarding the manner of its indication upon it: and this is what Fakhr al-Islam said: the fourth concerning the aspects of knowing the rulings of nazm.
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification text in Hanafi usul al-fiqh. The author expounds the fourfold division of speech (kalam) with respect to its relation to meaning, following the framework of Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi. The first division considers the original placement (wad') of words: words are either khass (specific), 'amm (general), or mushtarak (shared/ambiguous). The second division considers actual usage (isti'mal): whether a word is used in its original meaning or in a transferred sense. The third division considers clarity (zuhur) and obscurity (khafa') of meaning, including their various levels. The fourth division considers the manner of indication (kayfiyya dalala) of the word upon its meaning, which leads to understanding the rulings (ahkam) derived from that nazm (structured speech). The author notes that he has reversed the order of the second and third divisions compared to Fakhr al-Islam, because usage is logically prior to clarity and obscurity. This passage also implicitly indicates that the Qur'an is nazm dal 'ala al-ma'na (structured speech indicating meaning), and the author adopts this definition, which aligns with the Hanafi position as transmitted from Imam Abu Hanifa's later view.

Key Terms
نظم (nazm): Structured utterance; words arranged in a meaningful order
خاص (khass): A word placed for a specific, individual meaning
عام ('amm): A word that encompasses all individuals of its category without restriction
مشترك (mushtarak): A word shared between two or more meanings with equal probability

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many divisions of speech are there by consideration of its relation to meaning?
A) Two: khass and 'amm
B) Three: khass, 'amm, and mushtarak
C) Four: by wad', isti'mal, zuhur, and kayfiyya dalala
D) Five: adding Quran and hadith

Key Takeaway
Speech (kalam) in Hanafi usul is analyzed through four successive considerations: its original placement, its usage, its clarity, and its manner of indication, ultimately linking to the derivation of rulings.

Fourfold Division of Lafz: Wad', Isti'mal, and Dalalah
Explains fourfold division of lafz relative to meaning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four divisions of lafz relative to meaning, contrast the ordering views of Sadr al-Shariah and Fakhr al-Islam, and apply al-Taftazani's reconciliation to classify any lafz.

Core Idea
A word's meaning is determined by convention, usage, clarity, and mode of indication.

In Hanafi usul, understanding how a word relates to its meaning is foundational for deriving rulings. This lesson unpacks the fourfold classification of lafz: its original coinage (wad'), its usage (isti'mal), the clarity of its meaning (zuhur/khafa), and the precise mode by which it indicates that meaning (kayfiyyat al-dalalah). We will explore a subtle ordering debate between two Hanafi masters and see how later authorities resolved it by focusing on substance over labels.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three dimensions of lafz-ma'na relationship
The passage states that every word necessarily has a wad' (coining for a meaning), an isti'mal (usage in that meaning), and a dalalah (indication). These three dimensions yield four divisions: first (wad'), second (isti'mal), and under dalalah there are two: third (zuhur/khafa) and fourth (kayfiyyah).
This is the framework from Sadr al-Shariah in al-Tawdih.
2
Contrast the ordering of divisions by Fakhr al-Islam
Fakhr al-Islam al-Pazdawi placed the division by zuhur/khafa (clarity vs. obscurity) as the second division, and isti'mal as the third. Recognize that both scholars agree on the categories but differ in order.
The difference is pedagogical, not substantive.
3
Analyze the fourth division: kayfiyyat al-dalalah
The fourth division concerns the mode of indication: 'ibarah (explicit statement), isharah (implication), dalalah (connotation), and iqtida' (requirement). Understand each mode: 'ibarah is direct, isharah is indirect but understood, dalalah is necessary implication, iqtida' is an added element for validity.
These modes are crucial for extracting rulings from texts.
4
Examine the dispute over whether these modes belong to lafz or ma'na
Some scholars considered the fourth division as attributes of meaning (ma'na), others as attributes of the word (lafz). Al-Taftazani declares that all divisions are attributes of the lafz relative to meaning, and ignoring terminological variations is the practice of the mashayikh.
This reconciles the differing labels used by Fakhr al-Islam and Sadr al-Shariah.
5
Apply the framework to a concrete example
Consider the Quranic verse 'أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ'. Its wad' is the dictionary meaning of 'establish prayer'. Its isti'mal in Islamic law is the command to perform the ritual prayer. The zuhur/khafa: it is zahir (clear) as a command. The kayfiyyah: the 'ibarah indicates obligation; other modes (isharah, dalalah, iqtida') may derive further rulings like 'prayer must be on time' or 'prayer requires purity'.
This shows how all four divisions operate on one text.
